<leme no="238">

<section type="titlepage">

<page sig="a2r">

<heading>
<f type="r">THE
THEATRE OF
THE EARTH.
</heading>

Containing very short and compen&shy;
dious descriptions of all countries, gathered out
of the cheefest Cosmographers, both ancient and
moderne, and disposed in Alphabeticall order.

<f type="i">For the benefit of all such as delight to be acquainted
with the knowledge of strange countries, and the scituation
thereof, and especially for travellers, to whom the portabilitie of this
small volume will not be little commodious.<f type="r">

What is performed in this booke is more at large
set downe to the Readers view in the next leafe.

LONDON,
Printed for William Iones, and are to be sold
at his shop neare Holborne conduit at the 
signe of the Gun.
<f type="i">1601.<f type="r">

</section>
</page>

<page sig="a2v">
</page>

<page sig="a3r">
<section type="preface">
<heading>
What is performed in this Booke.
</heading>

THe cheefe countries in such sort described,
that the Reader may know their bounds,
and the countries on which they border,
East, South, West, and Northward.

2 The smaller countries, as shires and little
regions, declared by the more general, greater.
and better known countries and kingdoms in
which they are.

3 The riuers which are famous, either for
that by them countries are parted and divided
one from the other, or for some other cause wherein
they surpasse the rest, in such order set down, that
the countries wherein they be, or by which they
run, the heads whereout they rise, and the places
where they fall into the sea are made known and
declared.

4 The hills and mountains of fame in like
manner noted.
</page>

<page sig="a3v">
5 The seas, rocks, gulfs, Promontaries, or
headlands, bayes, and hauens deciphered.

6 The natures and conditions of divers peo&shy;
ple and nations expressed.

7 The cities of renown remembered.

8 To all Countries, Kingdomes, Isles, Cities,
Hils, Riuers, Seas, Gulfes, Bayes, Promontaries,
Hauend, not only both the ancient and moderne,
but also vulgar names adioined, for the Readers
better direction.

All done as briefely as the greatness of the
subject would permit.
</section>
</page>

<section type="dictionary">

<heading>
The Theatre of the Earth.
</heading>

<f type="i">Containing very short and compendious descriptions
of all countries, gathered out of the cheefest Cosmographers,
both ancient and moderne, and disposed in Alphabeticall order.<f type="r">

<wordgroup1 type="a"><alpha object="Ab">A B</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="un" lexeme="Abahius(pn)">ABahius,</form> <xpln lang="en">A name of
the riuer <f type="i">Nilus<f type="r">, as
being the father
of riuers, by reas<expan type="o+_">on</expan>
that by a long and win&shy;
ding course it spreadeth
it selfe into many crooks
and branches.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="un" lexeme="Abala(pn)">Abala,</form> <xpln lang="en">A hauen neere <f type="i"><term lang="un">Mes&shy;
salia</term><f type="r">. There is also a 
towne of this name by 
the red sea.</xpln>
<lemenote>
"Abala" occurs earlier in an English context in Appianus (1578).
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Abalites(pn)">Abalites,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="Abala(pn)">A Bay in the <f type="i">Tro&shy;
glodite<f type="r"> sea, so called of 
the hauen <f type="i">Abala<f type="r">.</xpln>

</lemenote>
</wordentry>

droppeth great store of

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Abatos(pn)">Abatos,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="Egypt(pn)" lexeme="Ethiopia(pn)" lexeme="Styx(pn)" lexeme="Memphis(pn)">An Isle of &AElig;gypt,
in the marish of Mem&shy;

was buried. This Isle was
so called, because that
no man could come to

and moorish slime, and

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Abij(pn)">Abij,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="Scythia(pn)">A people in Scythia,
they haue no sure place
to dwell in, and haue no
houses: They seeke for
their victuals when they
are hungry, making pro&shy;
uision of nothing, and
they liue in peace with&shy;
out war, content with 
their Country, and that 
which it affordeth them:
by reason whereof, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Ho&shy;
mer</term><f type="r"> calleth them most 
iust people. They be cal&shy;
led <f type="i"><term lang="un">Abij</term><f type="r">, either because
they liue in peace with&shy;
out warre, or for that 
they liue without ex&shy;
temporall diet, neuer 
prouiding for meat, vn&shy;
till they bee hungrie: for 
the Scythians for the 
most part are named ac&shy;
cording to their man&shy;
ners, as <f type="i"><term lang="la">Galactophagi</term><f type="r"> for 
eating of milke, and <f type="i"><term lang="la">An&shy;
tropophagi</term><f type="r"> for eating of 
mans flesh.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Abissini(pn)">Abissini,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="Ethiopia(pn)" lexeme="Egypt(pn)" lexeme="Red sea(pn)" lexeme="Manikongo(pn)" lexeme="Niger river(pn)" lexeme="Nubia(pn)" lexeme="Nile river(pn)">A people in &AElig;&shy;
thiopia, otherwise cal&shy;
led <f type="i"><term lang="la">Prestegiani</term><f type="r">, in the 
subiection of priest <f type="i">Iohn<f type="r">
whome they call in the 
&AElig;thiopian Tongue,
<f type="i"><term lang="et">Negusch Chauuariavni</term><f type="r">,
</page>

<page sig="b1r">
which is as much to say,
as an Apostolicke Em&shy;
perour, or most Christi&shy;
an king, who vndoub&shy;
tedly is accounted to be 
one of the cheefest Mo&shy;
narchs of our time. The 
borders of his kingdom
are by later Cosmogra&shy;
phers thus described, on
the North side it is ioi&shy;
ned to &AElig;gypt, on the 
East a part of it lieth on 
the red sea, on the South
it is by nature fenced
and compassed with the 
hils, <f type="i"><term lang="la">Montes lune</term><f type="r">, that is 
the hils of the Moone,
so called for the heighth:
and on the West it bor&shy;
dereth on the kingdome
Manicongo, the riuer
Niger, the kingdome of 
Nubia, and the riuer Ni&shy;
lus. The people of this
mightie Monarchie, are
called of the Arabians
<f type="i"><term lang="ar">Habasti</term><f type="r">, and of the La&shy;
times <f type="i"><term lang="la">Abasseni</term><f type="r">, and af&shy;
terwards <f type="i"><term lang="ar">Abissini</term><f type="r">; but 
they call themselues
Chaldeans, not without
great reason, conside&shy;
ring their tongue, in 
which the holie Scrip&shy;
tures are most elegant&shy;
ly and exquisitely writ&shy;
ten, little or nothing 
differing from the Chal&shy;
dean.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Africa(pn)">Africa,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="Lybia(pn)" lexeme="Hesperia(pn)" lexeme="Atlas hill(pn)" lexeme="Atlantic sea(pn)" lexeme="Mediterranean sea(pn)" lexeme="Barbaria(pn)" lexeme="Numidia(pn)" lexeme="greater Africa(pn)" lexeme="lesser Africa(pn)">The third part of

Barbaria, Numidia, Ly&shy;

led, either because they
bee blacke, or because

into two parts, greater
Affricke, and lesser Af&shy;
fricke: greater Affricke
is that part which be&shy;
ginneth at the South
and reacheth till the
West: lesser Affricke 
hath on the South <term lang="la">Nu&shy;
midia</term>, on the East <term lang="la">Cy&shy;
renaica</term>, and on the
North the Mediterane&shy;
an sea.</xpln></wordentry>

ria, on the North side it

<term lang="la">Caucasij</term>, and on the

menia. It hath beene vn&shy;
der diuerse kings, as the
Persians and Romanes,
and nowe is vnder the
great <f type="i">Cam<f type="r">, king of Tar&shy;
tarie. <f type="i">Strabo<f type="r"> reporteth,
that there were six and
twentie languages spo&shy;
ken in <term lang="la">Albania</term>. Scot&shy;
land in times past was
called <term lang="la">Albania</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Amazones(pn)">Amazones,</form> <xpln lang="en" lexeme="amazon(pn)" lexeme="Scythia(pn)">Warlike wo&shy;

so called because they
haue but one pap, or, as

flesh: and other hold
opinion, that they were

Nimph <f type="i">Amazonis<f type="r">. They
were women neere the
riuer <term lang="la">Thanais</term>, without
any men among them,
but least their number
should diminish, they
vsed to ha<expan type="u+_">un</expan>t their their neigh&shy;
bours countries til they
conceaued with child,
&amp; then returning home,
if it were a male child
they slew it, if a female,
they burnt the right pap
</page>

of it that it might(be&shy;
ing come to age) more 
readily and quickely vse
the right arme, with&shy;
out cumbrance.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Arcadia(pn)">Arcadia,</form> <xpln lang="en">A country in the
inmost part of <term lang="gkt">Pelopo&shy;
nesus</term>, so called of <f type="i">Arcas<f type="r">, 
sonne to <f type="i">Iupiter<f type="r"> by <f type="i">Cali&shy;
sto<f type="r"> the daughter of <f type="i">Ly&shy;
caon<f type="r">, king of <term lang="gkt">Arcadia</term>. it
was sometimes called 
<term lang="gkt">Pelasgia</term>, of that mighty 
and wise king <f type="i">Pelasgus<f type="r">.
It was also called <term lang="gkt">Lyca&shy;
onia, Gygantis</term>, &amp; <term lang="gkt">Par&shy;
rhasia</term>. And at last <f type="i">Arcas<f type="r">
ouercomming and van&shy;
quishing the <term lang="gkt">Pelasgi</term>, it
was of him named <term lang="gkt">Ar&shy;
cadia</term>. It hath threescore
and sixteene hils, besides
it hath huge and great
asses, insomuch that ne&shy;
uer any were found in
other countries of like
bignesse. In this country 
is the stone <f type="i">Asbeston<f type="r">
found, which burneth 
continually, and cannot 
be quenched. All <term lang="gkt">Arca&shy;
dia</term> was for the most part
inhabited by sheepe&shy;
heards, being full of pa&shy;
sture grounds, hauing 
little or no plowland at
all, neither is there anie
trafficke of merchandise
vsed in any part of the 
whole countrey. And 
the fore Pan was by the 
Arcadians challenged
to be their god, proper&shy;
ly and peculiarly. These
are the cheefest Hils of
<term lang="gkt">Arcadia</term>, <term lang="gkt">Pholoe</term>, <term lang="gkt">Lyl&shy;
lene</term>, <term lang="gkt">Lynceus</term>, <term lang="gkt">M&aelig;nalus</term>
and <term lang="gkt">Nonacris</term>: these the
cheefest cities &amp; towns,
<term lang="gkt">Mantinea</term>, <term lang="gkt">Tegea</term>, and
<term lang="gkt">Stymphalus</term> and these 
the cheefest Riuers,
<term lang="gkt">Ladon</term> and <term lang="gkt">Eryman&shy;
thus</term>.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Armenia(pn)">Armenia,</form> <xpln lang="en">A Countrey of
Asia, diuided into two
parts, into Armenia the 
greater, and Armenia
the lesser. The greater
Armenia hath on the
North <term lang="gkt">Colchis</term>, <term lang="gkt">Iberia</term>, 
<term lang="la">Albania</term>: on the West, 
part of Cappadocia: on
the East, the <term lang="un">Hercan</term> sea:
on the South, <term lang="gkt">Mesopo&shy;
tamia</term> and Assyria. The
lesser Armenia hath on
the North and West, 
part of Cappadocia, on 
the East Euphrates, on 
the South Taurus. In
Armenia are these hils,
the hils <term lang="tu">Moschici</term>, which 
are reared ouer a part of 
Pontus and Cappado&shy;
cia, <term lang="la">Pariedrus</term>, whence 
spring the Riuers Eu&shy;
phrates &amp; <term lang="am">Araxes</term>, <term lang="gkt">An&shy;
titaurus</term>, which running
</page>

<page sig="e1v">
out in length through
Media, was afterward
called <term lang="la">Albus</term>: <term lang="un">Cordica</term>, 
whence the riuer <term lang="pe">Ti&shy;
gris</term> springeth: Taurus
and <term lang="gkt">Niphates</term>, which 
separate <term lang="gkt">Mesopotamia</term>
and Assyria from <term lang="gkt">Ar&shy;
menia</term>. The hils <term lang="gkt">Caspij</term>,
which look toward <term lang="pe">Me&shy;
dia</term>: and the hils <term lang="gkt">Cau&shy;
casi</term>, which diuide In&shy;

are in Armenia foure ri&shy;

springing in the Hill 
<term lang="gkt">Caucasus</term>, leaueth Ibe&shy;
ria with <term lang="la">Albania</term> on the
left side, and Armenia
on the right, and so fal&shy;

<term lang="ar">Araxis</term>, <term lang="gkt">Phasis</term>, and <term lang="gkt">Ly&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="Asia(pn)">Asia,</form> <xpln lang="en">The third part of the
world, being as big as
both the other parts, to
wit Europe and Affrike:
it is diuided into two
parts, into Asia maior,
and Asia minor, greater
and lesser Asia: the grea&shy;
ter is parted from Eu&shy;
rope by Tanais, &amp; from 
Affricke by Nilus: the
lesser, now called Tur&shy;
</page>

<page sig="e2v">
kie and Natolia, hath in
it these realmes, Pontus,
Bithynia, Prygia, Gala&shy;
tia, Paphlagonia, Mag&shy;
nesia, Cappadocia, Ca&shy;
ryia, Lycia, Lydia, Lycao&shy;
nia, and Comagena.
Asia is by ancient Cos&shy;
mographers diuersly di&shy;
uided, and of late hath
beene by learned men
parted into fiue parts, 
according to the cheefe 
Empires of it. The first
part is all that which is
vnder the great Duke
of Muscouia, and is next 
to Europe. The second, 
is that part which is vn&shy;
der the great <f type="i">Cam<f type="r">, Em&shy;
perour of Tartarie. The
third is ruled by the Oc&shy;
tomanni or Turkes. The
fourth part is vnder the
Persians, who are now 
gouerned by the Sophi:
and the fift part contai&shy;
neth al the Indian coun&shy;
tries, which are gouer&shy;
ned by diuers kings and
princes. But all the pla&shy;
ces on the sea side from
the red sea vntil the Pro&shy;
montarie, commonlye 
called <f type="i"><term lang="it">Capo de Lampo</term><f type="r">,
are all almost vnder the
king of Portugal, or pay
tribute vnto him. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Athos(pn)">Athos,</form> <xpln lang="en">A high mountain

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Atlantic&aelig;(pn)">Atlantic&aelig;,</form> <xpln lang="en">Two isles called

temperate and pleasant, 
and the earth of it selfe
vntilled and vnlabored,
bringeth forth aboun&shy;
dance of delectable fruit;
wherefore the poets fai&shy;
ned the Elysian fields,
and the mansions of the
blessed to bee in these
isles, which they called
Hesperides. It is thought
that the isles Spagnola
and Cuba, are the same
isles, and were called <f type="i">At&shy;
lantic&aelig;<f type="r">. Cuba is an island 
of the Westerne newe 
world, adiacent to Asia.
The longitude and lati&shy;
tude of it is 269, 30.
</page>

<page sig="e4r">
23, 30. It is vnder the
tropicke of Cancer.
Cuba is also a cittie of 
India within Ganges.
And Spagnola is an 
island adioining to A&shy;
merica. The midst of it
containeth 305, 0. 23, 0.</xpln></wordentry>

rei</term><f type="r">.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="un" lexeme="Cefalo(pn)">Cefalo,</form> <xpln lang="en">An isle of Affricke,
found first in the yeare
1500. being three miles
in length, and one in 
bredth: It hath two vil&shy;
lages, and one strong 
castle. In the yeare 1505
the inhabitants rebelled
against the Portugals,
and chusing a king a&shy;
mong themselues, be&shy;
sieged a certaine castle
which the Portugals
held, insomuch, that the 
Portugals being in great
danger, and not know&shy;
ing how to escape, vpon
a certaine night (the Af&shy;
fricanes thining on no
such matter) issued out
of the castle, and direct&shy;
ly fell vpon the kings
Tent on the suddaine, 
where they slewe him
and a great company 
of his Noblemen, taking
his head with them, and 
set it on the top of their
wals vpon a Speare, and 
so escaped that great
danger, and created a 
new king, as Lieute&shy;
nant to the king of Por&shy;
tugall.</xpln></wordentry>

tuation, it excelleth all

the countrey about it is
pleasant and fertile, bea&shy;
ring great store of Oyle,
Dates, Citrons, Oliues, 
and all other kinds of 
fruits. It is in longitude
and latitude 5, 42. 37, 0.</xpln>

them by their proper 
names, which they fol&shy;
lowing, lose their way
and company, and not
knowing how to come

end. Others seeme to see
a mighty hoast of armed
men comming against
them, to shun which ar&shy;
mie, they step aside, and 
can neuer get in their
right way againe: and
diuerse such illusions &
deceits are vsed by euill
spirits in that Wood,
whereby many poore
trauellers haue most

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="un" lexeme="Moscovia(pn)" lexeme="Russia(pn)">Moscouia, <term lang="en">or rather</term> Russia,</form> <xpln lang="en">
The whole Empire of
the great Duke of Mos&shy;
couia, who calleth him&shy;
selfe Emperour of Rus&shy;
sia. It is limitted on the
North with the glaciale
or frosen sea, on the East
with Tartaria, on the
South with Turkie and
Polonia, and on the
West with Liuonia and 
Suetia. The people vseth
the Sclauonian tongue,
they bee christened, and
obserue the rites of the
Greekes, and in their ap&shy;
parell doe much agree
with the Hungarians.
This Countrey hath
great store of costly
furre, which is thence
carried in<emend err="r">t</emend>o all parts of 
the world.</xpln>
<lemenote>
"Moscouia" occurs earlier in an English context in Best (1578), "Russia" in Anghiera (1555), and "Suetia" in Allen (1581).

very sweet waters, but 
victuals are very deere,
for almost all things that
serueth for their suste&shy;
nance, are brought into 
the country from other
places. About three dais

great store of Pearle
cockles, which breede
fairer and greater Mar&shy;
garits, then any other
kind of pearle. This Ci&shy;
tie was wonne by the 
Lieutenant, generall of
the king of Portugals
Nauie, about the yeare
of Christ 1506, and for&shy;
ced to pay great tribute
yeerely to the kings of

longitude and latitude, 
96, 20. 19, 0.

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="un" lexeme="Pontus(pn)">Pontus,</form> <xpln lang="en">The sea betweene
M&aelig;otis and Tenedos.
The first streight whereof
next to Tenedos; is cal&shy;
led Hellespont, the place
where it is againe en&shy;
creased, is called Pro&shy;
pontis; the next streight
Thracius Bosphorus; &amp; 
where it is widest of all,
that is Pontus Euxinus;
which bowed in the 
form of a Scythian bow,
is ioined to the entrie or
mouth of the Meere
M&aelig;otis, which entrie is
called <term lang="gkt">Cimmerius Bos&shy;
phorus</term>. Figurately Pon&shy;
tus is taken for any sea.
Also a countrey of les&shy;
ser Asia, hauing on the 
West the riuer Halys,
on the East Colchis, on 
the South lesser Arme&shy;
nia, and on the North
Pontus Euxinus, part of 
the sea aboue described. 
<f type="i">Ptolomey<f type="r"> describeth it
thus, Pontus (sayth hee)
doth bound Westward
vpon the mouth of the
sea Pontus, and Thraci&shy;
us Bosphorus; South&shy;
ward vpon the countrey
which properly is called
Asia; and Northward
vpon part of Pontus Eu&shy;
xinus.</xpln>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Roma(pn)">Roma,</form> <xpln lang="en">The famous citty of
Rome, cheefe Citty of
Italie, and sometimes al&shy;
most head of the whole
world, built by <f type="i">Romulus<f type="r">
and <f type="i">Rhemus<f type="r">, the ne&shy;
phewes of <f type="i">Numitor<f type="r">:
some hold opinion, that
it was built many ages
before <f type="i">Romulus<f type="r">, and cal&shy;
led Valentia, and that it
beg<expan type="a+_">an</expan> to be named Rome
after the comming of
<f type="i">Euander<f type="r"> and <f type="i">&AElig;neas<f type="r"> to 
Italie. The longitude &
latitude, 36, 40. 42, 0.
The Empire of Rome
first reached vntill Por&shy;
tus and Hostia in Italie,
containing in all but
eighteene myles in 
length, and so continued
243 yeares, vnder the
gouernement of seuen
kings, <f type="i">Romulus<f type="r">, <f type="i">Numa 
Pompilius<f type="r">, <f type="i">Tullus Hosti&shy;
lius<f type="r">, <f type="i">Ancus Martius<f type="r">, <f type="i">Tar&shy;
quinius Priscus<f type="r">, <f type="i">Seruius 
</page>

<page sig="aa2v">
Tullus<f type="r">, and <f type="i">Tarquinius
Superbus<f type="r">. Afterward it 
was gouerned by Con&shy;
suls 447 yeares, among
whom sometimes were
Dictators, Decemuiri, 
and militarie Tribunes.
Vnder the Consuls all
Italie, till beyond Padus,
was ioined to the Em&shy;
pire of Rome. Affricke 
and Spaine was subdu&shy;
ed, France and Brittaine
made tributarie, the Il&shy;
lyrici, Histri, Liburni,
Dalmat&aelig; vanquished,
Achaia inuaded, Mace&shy;
donie taken, the Darda&shy;
ni, Moesi, and Thracians
fought withall, Danubi&shy;
us reached, Asia entred,
Antiochus driv<expan type="e+_">en</expan> away,
the kingdome of Pon&shy;
tus (<f type="i">Mithridates<f type="r"> being 
ouercome) obtained,
with lesser Armenia,
which he also held, Me&shy;
sopotamia not left vn&shy;
touched, a league made
with the Parthians, the 
Cardueni, Saraceni, and
Arabians assaulted, Iu&shy;
dea conquered, and Ci&shy;
licia and Syrsa brought 
in suiection. Thus was
the Empire vnder the
Consuls enlarged vntill
&AElig;gypt. Lastly, vnder the 
Emperours from <f type="i">Au&shy;
gustus<f type="r">, till the times of
<f type="i">Theodosius<f type="r"> and his sons
<f type="i">Honorius<f type="r"> and <f type="i">Arcadius<f type="r">,
containing 440 yeares,
the Cantabri, Astures,
all Spaine, all the Alpes,
Noricum, Pannonia,
and M&oelig;sia, was made 
subiect to the Empire:
All the countrey about
Danubius, all Pontus,
and greater Armenia,
Mesopotamia, Assyria,
Arabia, and &AElig;gypt. And
in this manner was the
Empire of Rome by the
vertue and prowesse of 
the Romanes brought
to the top of heigth,
euen to their neuer dy&shy;
ing glory. The limits and
borders thereof were,
on the West the Ocean,
</page>

<page sig="aa3r">
on the North the riuer 
Rheyne and Danubius,
on the East <term lang="pe">Tygris</term>, and
on the South the hill
Atlas.</xpln>
<lemenote>
This page is supplied from the 1599 edition (STC 23931).
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Sarmatia(pn)">Sarmatia,</form> <xpln lang="en">A large country 
reaching from the very
borders of Germanie,
and the riuer Vistula to
Hircania, and is diuided
into Sarmatia Europ&aelig;a,
and Asiatica. Sarmatia
Europ&aelig;a hath on the
North part of the Occe&shy;
an, of it called Oceanus,
Sarmaticus; on the West,
part of Germanie, and
the riuer Vistula; on the
South the people Iazi&shy;
ges Metanast&aelig;, and on
the East it is parted from
Sarmatia Asiatica, by 
the riuer Tanais. Sarma&shy;
tia Asiatica on the East
boundeth vpon Hirca&shy;
nia, on the North vpon 
the vnknowne land, on 
the West vpon the riuer 
Tanais, by which it is 
parted from the other
Sarmatia, and on the
South vpon part of Pon&shy;
tus Euxinus. The people
of both the one and the
other Sarmatia, are very
wild and fierce, liuing
either in desarts and so&shy;
litarie places, as hils, 
forrests, and woods, or
vsing carts in steede of
houses to dwell in: they 
are some of the them fed
with mares milke, some 
with raw meate mixed
with horse bloud, and
some of them with m<expan type="a+_">an</expan>s
flesh. They that are a&shy;
mong them giuen to
warres, vse to sucke the
wounds of them that
are slaine, and to drinke 
their bloud, yea to shred
them in peeces, and feed
on their flesh, wherefore
they bee called Anthro&shy;
pophagi. In a word some
of the Sarmat&aelig; bee the
wildest and most brutish 
sort of p<emend err="a">e</emend>ople that is in 
the world.</xpln></wordentry>

</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="un" lexeme="Scythia(pn)">Scythia,</form> <xpln lang="en">A Countrey in the

the other runneth along

Scythia according to
<f type="i">Ptolomey<f type="r">, is parted by the
hill Imaus, and so diui&shy;

Imaus, &amp; Scythia with&shy;

dome of Cathay dooth
nowe containe all Scy&shy;
thia. <f type="i">Ortelius<f type="r"> thinketh, 
That Scythia within
Imaus, containeth a 
great part of Tartarie.
And <f type="i">Niger<f type="r"> reporteth,
</page>

<page sig="cc1r">
that that part of Scy&shy;
thia which is without
Imaus, is by the barba&shy;
rous people called <f type="i">Mon&shy;
gal<f type="r">, and of late <f type="i"><term lang="la">Antiqua
Tartaria</term><f type="r">, ancient Tarta&shy;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la" lexeme="Turc&aelig;(pn)">Turc&aelig;,</form> <xpln lang="en">The people nowe

and in the end wasted
&amp; subdued Persia, Pon&shy;
tus, and Cappadocia,
and hauing all lesser A&shy;
sia, they set footing in 
Europe, a part whereof 
with all the rest, they
hold euen till this day,
insomuch that now the
Empire of the Turke
reacheth from Sicilie
Eastward to the confines
of Persia, Northward to
Tartarie, &amp; Southward
